    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<span id="freeTextContainer10505099236596371543">The Brothers Lionheart (Swedish: Bröderna Lejonhjärta) is a children's fantasy novel written by Astrid Lindgren. It was published in the autumn of 1973 and has been translated into 46 languages. Many of its themes are unusually dark and heavy for the children's book genre. Disease, death, tyranny, betrayal and rebellion are some of the dark themes that permeate the story. </span>
  <span id="freeText10505099236596371543" style="display:none">The Brothers Lionheart (Swedish: Bröderna Lejonhjärta) is a children's fantasy novel written by Astrid Lindgren. It was published in the autumn of 1973 and has been translated into 46 languages. Many of its themes are unusually dark and heavy for the children's book genre. Disease, death, tyranny, betrayal and rebellion are some of the dark themes that permeate the story. The lighter themes of the book involve platonic love, loyalty, hope, courage and pacifism.<br /><br />The two main characters are two brothers; the older Jonatan and the younger Karl. The two brothers' surname was originally Lion, but they are generally known as Lionheart. Karl's nickname is Skorpan (Rusky) since Jonatan likes these typical Swedish toasts or crusts.<br /><br />In Nangijala, a land in "the campfires and storytelling days", the brothers experience adventures. Together with a resistance group they lead the struggle against the evil Tengil, who rules with the aid of the fearsome fire-breathing dragon, Katla.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10505099236596371543" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_977313555" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/977313555" />
    <a title="Lisa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32532774-lisa"><img alt="Lisa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1480492067p2/32532774.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/977313555?book_show_action=true">Jun 25, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lisa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lisa" href="/user/show/32532774-lisa">Lisa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9789129640243" href="/book/show/1888503.Br_derna_Lejonhj_rta">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32532774-lisa?shelf=astrid-lindgren">astrid-lindgren</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32532774-lisa?shelf=children">children</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer977313555" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1308902000382643080">There are times when reality is hard to bear!<br/><br/>There are times when you wish you would not have to face what is actually happening. For all those times, Astrid Lindgren wrote The Brothers Lionheart. I must have read it about ten times by now, several times as a child, and several times with my own children, and students. I have watched the film, listened to the magical audiobook in which Astrid Lindgren herself reads the story, in that slow, humoristic voice of hers, indicating her Småland dialect</span>
  <span id="freeText1308902000382643080" style="display:none">There are times when reality is hard to bear!<br /><br />There are times when you wish you would not have to face what is actually happening. For all those times, Astrid Lindgren wrote The Brothers Lionheart. I must have read it about ten times by now, several times as a child, and several times with my own children, and students. I have watched the film, listened to the magical audiobook in which Astrid Lindgren herself reads the story, in that slow, humoristic voice of hers, indicating her Småland dialect ever so little. And I have read quite a few reflections on the book, as well - mostly discussions about whether or not she was right to break the taboo of death in a children's adventure book.<br /><br />What remains with me are two things: the power of storytelling to make life bearable, and the recurring pattern of human society, regardless of plot, setting, characters and purpose of the story.<br /><br />The first chapter is of the kind that makes you cry helplessly: a poor boy with a deadly illness, probably tuberculosis, overhears his mother talking about his expected death. Devastated, he confides in his older brother Jonathan, who reassures him that there is a wonderful adventurous life after death, in Nangijala, and that he will just be waiting there until Jonathan joins him. Things develop differently, however, and Jonathan dies himself, trying to save his brother from a fire. And shortly afterwards, the boys reunite in the wonderful fairytale world of Nangijala. So far, so kitsch.<br /><br />But of course Nangijala is a dark place as well, with a village behind a wall, reigned by the evil Tengil and his soldiers, supported by a superpower weapon, a dragon called Katla. Nobody will be surprised to hear that the novel was published at the height of the Cold War.<br /><br />I still feel a shiver down my spine when I think of the boys sneaking in behind enemy lines, using the password:<br /><br />All makt åt Tengil, vår befriare!" "All power to Tengil, our liberator!"<br /><br />Is it not a universal habit of tyrants that they proclaim themselves liberators? From what, I would like to ask: from freedom of movement? Freedom of choice? Peace?<br /><br />In the black and white world of Astrid Lindgren's fairytale, the brothers set out to save their world out of the glorious conviction that there are things you have to do, regardless of the danger you encounter:<br /><br />“Men då sa Jonatan att det fanns saker som man måste göra, även om det var farligt. ’Varför då’, undrade jag. ’Annars är man ingen människa utan bara en liten lort’, sa Jonatan.” <br />'I don't know if it was such a good thing to do,' said Jonathan. 'But there are things you have to do, otherwise you're not a human being, just a piece of dirt.'<br /><br />They win in the end, and the evil powers of Nangijala are defeated, but at a cost: Jonathan was hurt by Katla. <br /><br />And this is where the story loses its magical power and credibility for me, and where I feel Astrid Lindgren does not face up to reality, even if it is just the reality you find in a story universe. <br /><br />The boys decide to commit suicide to move on to the next world, Nangilima, where the stories are better and life is easier. In a chain of eternal worlds, there can be multiple happy (or sad) endings. But I don't particularly like that message, and have always found it hard to convey to my children. Not because of the theme of death, but because of the careless attitude towards the reality we currently live in and can't stomach.<br /><br />If we just move on whenever life does not turn out the way we expect it, and move from adventure to adventure, ignoring the fallout of the reality we dodge by seeking an easy way out, there is no real incentive to change the premises of the world we live in for the better, or to try to figure out the reasons for the problems we have, in order to prevent them from getting worse. <br /><br />Close your eyes and jump!<br /><br />I always thought that was contradictory to the message that you are a piece of dirt if you do not do what you think is right. <br /><br />But then again, Astrid Lindgren might have seen clearly what humanity does all the time: building walls, fighting over ideology, ignoring bad signs, denying reality and jumping blindly into the future, hoping for the best without ever turning around to learn from history, believing in an afterlife that will be different and better, despite being populated by the same set of characters that populate earth.<br /><br />There is one beautiful idea expressed in the story, that I love reading over and over again:<br /><br />"Men jag kan inte döda någon’, sa Jonatan, ’det vet du, Orvar!’ […] ’Om alla vore som du’, sa Orvar, ’då skulle ju ondskan få regera i all evinnerlighet!’ Men då sa jag att om alla vore som Jonatan, så skulle det inte finnas någon ondska.<br /><br />“But I can't kill anyone,' said Jonathan, 'you know that Orvar!' 'If everyone were like you,' said Orvar, 'then evil would rule for all eternity!' But then I said that if everyone were like Jonathan, then there would be no evil.” <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1308902000382643080" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer746475315" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13179164139973703870">Bröderna Lejonhjärta = The Brothers Lionheart, Astrid Lindgren <br/>The Brothers Lionheart is a children's fantasy novel written by Astrid Lindgren. It was published in the autumn of 1973 and has been translated into 46 languages. Many of its themes are unusually dark and heavy for the children's book genre. Disease, death, tyranny, betrayal and rebellion are some of the dark themes that permeate the story. The lighter themes of the book involve platonic love, loyalty, hope, courage and pacifism. In </span>
  <span id="freeText13179164139973703870" style="display:none">Bröderna Lejonhjärta = The Brothers Lionheart, Astrid Lindgren <br />The Brothers Lionheart is a children's fantasy novel written by Astrid Lindgren. It was published in the autumn of 1973 and has been translated into 46 languages. Many of its themes are unusually dark and heavy for the children's book genre. Disease, death, tyranny, betrayal and rebellion are some of the dark themes that permeate the story. The lighter themes of the book involve platonic love, loyalty, hope, courage and pacifism. In an unnamed Swedish city, ten year-old Karl Lejon has found out that he is going to die from an unspecified pulmonary disease. His adored big brother, 13-year-old Jonatan, calms him down and tells him that in the afterlife, all men will go to a land known as Nangijala. One day, a fire breaks out in the Lejon home. Jonatan takes Karl on his back and jumps out of the house's window to save him, but dies himself in the fall. Karl is crestfallen over his brother's death, until, just before his own demise, he receives a sign which allays his fears of death, and when he wakes again, he finds himself in the Cherry Valley of Nangijala, where he is happily reunited with Jonatan. Karl is introduced to the denizens of the valley, particularly Sofia the dove-keeper, Hubert the hunter, and Jossi, a landlord, and assumes the surname Lionheart along with his brother. ...<br />عنوانها: ب‍رادران‌ ش‍ی‍ردل‌؛ دره‌ گ‍ل‌ س‍رخ‌؛ نویسنده: آس‍ت‍ری‍د ل‍ی‍ن‍دگ‍رن‌؛ تاریخ نخستین خوانش: سال 1990 میلادی<br />عنوان: ب‍رادران‌ ش‍ی‍ردل‌؛ نویسنده: آس‍ت‍ری‍د ل‍ی‍ن‍دگ‍رن‌؛ مت‍رج‍م: اک‍ب‍ر گ‍ل‍رن‍گ‌؛ تهران، مردمک؛ 1368؛ در 289 ص؛ چاپ دیگر: تهران، پیک فرهنگ؛ 1368؛ در 289 ص؛ 1370، چاپ سوم 1375؛ موضوع: داستانهای نویسندگان سوئدی - سده 20 م<br />عنوان: دره‌ گ‍ل‌ س‍رخ‌؛ نویسنده: آس‍ت‍ری‍د ل‍ی‍ن‍دگ‍رن‌؛ مت‍رج‍م: ع‍زی‍زال‍ل‍ه‌ ق‍وطاس‍ل‍و؛ تهران، کتاب جوانه، 1370؛ در 240 ص، مصور؛ چاپ دیگر: ت‍ه‍ران‌: دف‍ت‍ر ن‍ش‍ر ف‍ره‍ن‍گ‌ اس‍لام‍ی‌‏‫، 1375؛ چاپ دیگر: ‏‫تهران ‬‏‫: شرکت انتشارات سوره مهر‬‏‫‬‏، 1393؛ شابک: 9786001751493؛ ‬<br />‬عنوان: ب‍رادران‌ ش‍ی‍ردل‌؛ نویسنده: اس‍ت‍ری‍د ل‍ی‍ن‍دگ‍رن‌؛ م‍ت‍رج‍م‌: م‍ح‍م‍د زری‍ن‌ب‍ال‌؛ ت‍ه‍ران‌: ن‍ش‍ر ن‍ق‍طه‌، گ‍روه‌ ک‍ودک‌ و ن‍وج‍وان‌‏‫، 1375، در 234 ص؛ شابک: 9645548683؛‬<br />عنوان: ب‍رادران‌ ش‍ی‍ردل‌؛ نویسنده: اس‍ت‍ری‍د ل‍ی‍ن‍دگ‍رن‌؛ م‍ت‍رج‍م‌: بهمن رستم‌آبادی؛ ت‍ه‍ران‌: زنبق: فرهنگ و هنر‏‫، 1390؛ در 303 ص؛ شابک: 9789647501262؛ چاپ دوم 1397؛<br />عنوان: برادران شیردل؛ نویسنده: آسترید لیندگرن؛ مترجم از زبان سوئدی: ج‍ون‌ ت‍ی‍ت‌؛ مترجم: کمال اکرمی؛ زیر نظر شورای بررسی رمان؛ ویراستار پژمان واسعی؛ تهران: ‏‫محراب قلم، کتابهای مهتاب‏‫، 1397؛ ‬در 273 ص؛ شابک: 9786004132237؛<br />داستان دو برادر به نام‌های «یوناتان» و «کارل» است. «کارل»، برادر کوچکتر، و کودکی بیمار است، که از لحاظ عاطفی به برادر بزرگتر خود بسیار وابسته است. «کارل» به‌طور اتفاقی پی می‌برد، که به زودی خواهد مرد؛ «یوناتان» برای دلداری دادن به او، از سرزمینی زیبا، و افسانه‌ ای به نام «نانگیالا» سخن می‌گوید که آدم‌ها پس از مرگ به آنجا می‌روند. کمی بعد در یک آتش‌سوزی، «یوناتان» می‌میرد. «کارل» که پس از مرگ «یوناتان» زندگی خودش را غم‌انگیز می‌بیند، بی‌صبرانه منتظر است، تا او هم به «نانگیالا» برود، و در کنار «یوناتان» قرار بگیرد. او که بیمار است، پس از مدتی به او می‌پیوندد، و در «درهٔ گیلاس»، زندگی سرشار از آرامشی را با برادرش آغاز می‌کند. اما به زودی متوجه می‌شود در سرزمینی به نام درهٔ گل سرخ، که در همسایگی آنها واقع شده‌ است، شخص بی‌رحم و زورگویی به نام «تنگیل» حکومت می‌کند، که آرامش و آزادی را از مردمانش سلب کرده‌ است. او در می‌یابد که برادرش در این مدت با همراهی اهالی این سرزمین، مبارزاتی را در برابر حاکم زورگو آغاز کرده‌ است. او که برادر خود را سمبل شجاعت می‌داند، خواسته و ناخواسته پا به پای او در مسیر مبارزه با شر و پلیدی مبارزه می‌کند. ا. شربیانی</span>

<div id="review_233315307" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/233315307" />
    <a title="Helle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6909470-helle"><img alt="Helle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1430669590p2/6909470.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/233315307?book_show_action=true">Nov 09, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Helle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Helle" href="/user/show/6909470-helle">Helle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6909470-helle?shelf=books-i-own">books-i-own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6909470-helle?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6909470-helle?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6909470-helle?shelf=children-s-literature">children-s-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6909470-helle?shelf=read-aloud-to-kids">read-aloud-to-kids</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6909470-helle?shelf=swedish">swedish</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer233315307" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5618212532731918039">This is the most important, most magical and most precious book from my childhood. It is the book that made me believe in something greater than myself when I was about ten years old. It is, to me, one of the most wonderful stories for children and young teenagers. <br/><br/>By the time I was 18, I had read it three or four times, later I read it to my oldest daughter and now to my youngest. And I still struggled to hold back the tears.<br/><br/>It is about the love between two brothers, about life after death, abo</span>
  <span id="freeText5618212532731918039" style="display:none">This is the most important, most magical and most precious book from my childhood. It is the book that made me believe in something greater than myself when I was about ten years old. It is, to me, one of the most wonderful stories for children and young teenagers. <br /><br />By the time I was 18, I had read it three or four times, later I read it to my oldest daughter and now to my youngest. And I still struggled to hold back the tears.<br /><br />It is about the love between two brothers, about life after death, about believing in goodness and fighting evil – though that is what I see as an adult now. When I was a child (and to my own children), it was/is simply a magnificent and magical story which had me reading into the small hours of the night, but above all it is about hope. With this book alone Astrid Lindgren made an indelible imprint on my childhood. <br /><br />Years ago, I taught a course on fantasy fiction during which I had long discussions with my students about the genre. My students were educating themselves to become future (English) teachers, and the fantasy genre is a must in that connection, at least here in Denmark where it has been popular for a long time. A school teacher I knew at the time had read this story aloud to her pupils in school and had made sure to tell the children not to throw themselves from tall buildings because there was no such thing as Nangijala – the magical place that Karl and Jonatan go to after they die (early on in the book, so no spoiler). I made it my mission to tell my students never to do any such thing; children aren’t stupid. They know how to distinguish between what’s real and what isn’t. But more importantly: how did she know there is no Nangijala? To take away the hope and the magic that such a story offers is to misunderstand what the genre offers, not least this book.<br /><br />Shortly afterwards, my mother died quite unexpectedly. Among the many cards I received was one from those same students, in which they sent me their heartfelt condolences and which ended by their assuring me that ‘we believe in Nangijala.’ So even as an adult, this book is among those I will defend with my life and recommend to anyone who’ll listen. I know I’m not the only person to feel this way about <i>The Brothers Lionheart</i>, indeed I believe it’s part of the landscape of childhood for many Scandinavians, which just underlines how wondrous and magical Astrid Lindgren was.<br /><br /> <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5618212532731918039" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_82881275" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/82881275" />
    <a title="Manybooks" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2970379-manybooks"><img alt="Manybooks" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1459773375p2/2970379.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/82881275?book_show_action=true">Feb 01, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Manybooks" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Manybooks" href="/user/show/2970379-manybooks">Manybooks</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2970379-manybooks?shelf=book-reviews">book-reviews</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2970379-manybooks?shelf=childrens-literature">childrens-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2970379-manybooks?shelf=translations">translations</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2970379-manybooks?shelf=siblings">siblings</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2970379-manybooks?shelf=fairy-tales-fantasy">fairy-tales-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2970379-manybooks?shelf=death">death</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2970379-manybooks?shelf=astrid-lindgren">astrid-lindgren</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2970379-manybooks?shelf=death-loving">death-loving</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2970379-manybooks?shelf=evil">evil</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2970379-manybooks?shelf=loss">loss</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer82881275" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4442848281961122804">Well and truly, the first time I read Astrid Lindgren’s 1973 fantasy novel <u>The Brothers Lionheart</u> in 1977 (titled <i>Bröderna Lejonhärta</i> in the Swedish original, although yes, I was reading the novel in German translation) I not only found <u>The Brothers Lionheart</u> (<i>Die Brüder Löwenherz</i>) a diverting and entertaining reading experience, I in fact and indeed just loved loved loved absolutely everything about the author’s, about Astrid Lindgren’s narrative (and in particular I was also completely in awe </span>
  <span id="freeText4442848281961122804" style="display:none">Well and truly, the first time I read Astrid Lindgren’s 1973 fantasy novel <u>The Brothers Lionheart</u> in 1977 (titled <i>Bröderna Lejonhärta</i> in the Swedish original, although yes, I was reading the novel in German translation) I not only found <u>The Brothers Lionheart</u> (<i>Die Brüder Löwenherz</i>) a diverting and entertaining reading experience, I in fact and indeed just loved loved loved absolutely everything about the author’s, about Astrid Lindgren’s narrative (and in particular I was also completely in awe at Lindgren’s bravery approaching in a children’s novel subjects and topics like death, grief, loss and the concept that even in the so-called afterlife, danger and evil can reign supreme and hold sway). <br /><br />And indeed, first and foremost, what I have always most appreciated in <u>The Brothers Lionheart</u> is not only the incredibly close and loving personal relationship between strong and healthy older brother Jonathan and his sickly and dying of tuberculosis younger brother Karl (since due to major differences of temperament and likes/dislikes, my own relationship with my two siblings has sadly never been all that close) but also that this absolute love and affection extends even to the afterlife, even to Nangijala (and that therefore, when Jonathan and Karl once they are both residing in Nangijala realise that their at first glance idyllic seeming existence there is in fact gravely threatened, they as a brave two person team decide to actively fight against the tyranny and evil of Tengil and his weapon of mass destruction, the dragon Katla). <br /><br />Now if I were to rank <i>The Brothers Lionheart</i> only and solely from the perspective of my eleven year old self (from when I read the novel in 1977), I would most definitely and without any doubt whatsoever be choosing a solid five star ranking. However, I do have to admit that rereading <u>The Brothers Lionheart</u> as an older and much more critical adult, albeit that I do still very much enjoy Astrid Lindgren’s presented text (with Jonathan and Karl’s sweet sibling devotion with their bravery and fortitude combatting Tengil and Katla), I am sorry to say that I also tend to consider it somewhat uncomfortable and problematic that Astrid Lindgren seems to (at least to older adult I) be depicting death and the afterlife as almost something preferable to life (in so far that everything about Nangijala, even when it is threatened by evil and with possible destruction is still in my opinion depicted by Astrid Lindgren as somehow being better in every way than life on earth). And that after Jonathan is severely injured by in the battle with Katla, that the two brothers actually choose to commit suicide, and thereby to move from Nanjigala to Nangilama, where there supposedly is an easier existence with better stories, well, I just do not particularly appreciate as an adult reader the potential message this might give to children (that suicide is both acceptable and a not even all that traumatic escape, for while it might well be important to show children that death is not some monster to always and continuously fear, I do think that the way death is actively embraced and seen as a positive escape into an even more idyllic existence by Astrid Lindgren at the end of <u>The Brothers Lionheart</u>, this does go just a wee bit too far for my level of comfort).</span>
  <a data-text-id="4442848281961122804" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer207749747" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer2399960767183573095">Oh man, I am an absolute sucker for sibling relationships (have I mentioned this before? Maybe I have), if not, know now that I am. <br/><br/>So you might guess why this is (along with 'Mio, My Mio') my favourite Astrid Lindgren book. The relationship between Tvebak and Jonathan (the Danish names) is so unbelievably precious, and leaves me in tears, even now, despite it being a childrens book. <br/>Having a brother myself, I understand the devotion you can have for a sibling; I'd do anything for him. I'd face </span>
  <span id="freeText2399960767183573095" style="display:none">Oh man, I am an absolute sucker for sibling relationships (have I mentioned this before? Maybe I have), if not, know now that I am. <br /><br />So you might guess why this is (along with 'Mio, My Mio') my favourite Astrid Lindgren book. The relationship between Tvebak and Jonathan (the Danish names) is so unbelievably precious, and leaves me in tears, even now, despite it being a childrens book. <br />Having a brother myself, I understand the devotion you can have for a sibling; I'd do anything for him. I'd face my own death if it meant saving him, and I wouldn't even hesitate. Because I love him more than anything and nothing else is as important. <br /><br />I love this book, and I have loved it since I read it for the first time as a kid. It's a wonder of a children's book, really a masterpiece, handling some very difficult themes, and many of the exact things that I value so highly and have struggled with myself: trust, freedom, fighting for what you believe in, loyalty, and keeping the ones you love safe, whatever the cost. And this presents all of these things in a lovely, heartbreaking and hopeful tale. <br /><br />There's no doubt I'll be reading this to my own children some day. </span>
  <a data-text-id="2399960767183573095" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1506617214" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1506617214" />
    <a title="Leni Iversen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36264060-leni-iversen"><img alt="Leni Iversen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1414182808p2/36264060.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1506617214?book_show_action=true">Jan 12, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Leni Iversen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Leni Iversen" href="/user/show/36264060-leni-iversen">Leni Iversen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 8252536476" href="/book/show/11317832-br-drene-l-vehjerte">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36264060-leni-iversen?shelf=classics-bingo-2016">classics-bingo-2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36264060-leni-iversen?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36264060-leni-iversen?shelf=children">children</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1506617214" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7330569777466361986">Reading childhood favourites again as an adult is risky business, and with Astrid Lindgren it can be devastating. Not because her books disappoint; they don't. She has the gift of writing books that are read one way by children and another by adults. For a child, The Brothers Lionheart is an empowering adventure about brotherly love, courage, and doing the right thing even if you are scared. Children take the story at face value. It's the story of a sick child who gets to go on adventures and be</span>
  <span id="freeText7330569777466361986" style="display:none">Reading childhood favourites again as an adult is risky business, and with Astrid Lindgren it can be devastating. Not because her books disappoint; they don't. She has the gift of writing books that are read one way by children and another by adults. For a child, The Brothers Lionheart is an empowering adventure about brotherly love, courage, and doing the right thing even if you are scared. Children take the story at face value. It's the story of a sick child who gets to go on adventures and be healthy, and be a hero in his own little way. It's a story about not needing to fear death. I have heard of this book being used in therapy sessions with terminally ill children. It was a strange experience to read the book to my daughter and watch her react to it in the same way I had as a child, while I was now experiencing a different book. <br /><br />Yes, I found the plot transparent, and there were some convenient but unrealistic plot devices. But that's fair enough. It works for kids, and doesn't underestimate them. I also realised that it wasn't just a story of good versus evil, it was obviously a story of fascism, occupation and resistance. Older brother Jonathan keeps getting compared to a fairytale prince, but he is more like a young Christ-figure. This realisation didn't detract from the story at all, it just made it a different experience.<br /><br />What's more important is that I had trouble keeping my voice steady during the opening chapters, and had to take a break to wipe my tears before I could read on. Being a mother now, I identified not with the dying child, but with the mother. First her husband is lost at sea, then she loses both her children. My daughter looked at me and said, "But mummy, she will see them again in Nangijala!"<br /><br />The story felt, in part, like the hope and innocence of my childhood being stripped away. Maybe it wouldn't have affected me so much if I hadn't experienced it as a child first. As an adult you realise immediately that Nangijala isn't real, it is an invention to soothe a dying child. And that changes the whole book. Especially, it changes the ending. Once again I had trouble keeping my voice steady, but to my daughter it was a happy ending. Astrid Lindgren wrote for children, and she was always on their side. Champion of children, devastator of the grown-up heart. What an amazing literary gift.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7330569777466361986" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1255781191" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1255781191" />

  <span id="freeText2388320087251268488" style="display:none">This book is everything lovely about childhood to me. Just picking it up and looking at that well read cover and seeing the handwriting of my eight year self, I knew this was going to be as good today as then. Even though I know the story by heart, and I have seen the series/movie a ton of times, it still has something fresh and still timeless over it. <br /><br />At least in our part of the world Astrid Lindgren is cherished and loved for her childrens’ books. She’s mostly known for Pippi Longstocking, but I happen to think that this one is her best. It is like a fairytale and at the same time it is also so relevant for all ages. We all have to deal with loosing someone at some point in our life.<br /><br />This book tell the tale of two brothers each brave in their own way. It is a story of loss, grief and courage. It is a story of love between the closest of siblings and a love that endures no matter the circumstances. Maybe that is why I love it so much.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer255226056" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4724699274017029539">A strange if charming little book. It begins with the tragic deaths of two young brothers who are then reunited in the land of Ningiyama, where the world is still as it was in the age of sagas with many adventures to be had. However, the boys discover that not all adventures are worth having as they find themselves facing increasingly evil foes and long odds. The end of the story took me by surprise and I'm not sure what to make of it. Despite what a one-star review on this site claims, the endi</span>
  <span id="freeText4724699274017029539" style="display:none">A strange if charming little book. It begins with the tragic deaths of two young brothers who are then reunited in the land of Ningiyama, where the world is still as it was in the age of sagas with many adventures to be had. However, the boys discover that not all adventures are worth having as they find themselves facing increasingly evil foes and long odds. The end of the story took me by surprise and I'm not sure what to make of it. Despite what a one-star review on this site claims, the ending actually was foreshadowed earlier on and the world after Ningiyama was named by the elder brother Lionheart. Still, it's a strange way to end a children's story and I'd like to know what an actual 10-year old reader (the suggested lowest age on my copy of this book) would make of it. </span>

<div id="review_1077864913" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1077864913" />
    <a title="Tandava Brahmachari" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14426279-tandava-brahmachari"><img alt="Tandava Brahmachari" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1352486560p2/14426279.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1077864913?book_show_action=true">Oct 11, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tandava Brahmachari" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tandava Brahmachari" href="/user/show/14426279-tandava-brahmachari">Tandava Brahmachari</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 0141310812" href="/book/show/23356226-the-brothers-lionheart">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14426279-tandava-brahmachari?shelf=middle-grade">middle-grade</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14426279-tandava-brahmachari?shelf=spiritual-fiction">spiritual-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14426279-tandava-brahmachari?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1077864913" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4821908249067191515">For years I've had a strong but vaguely-detailed memory of a book I read as a child. I couldn't remember the title, the author, the plot, or even how old I was when I read it. I knew that a boy followed his brother to a land after death, and I had a blurry image of what was possibly the cover, and I knew that younger-me had been deeply moved by it in some way. Every few years I'd try to find it, but without any luck. <br/><br/>A few weeks ago, I found it. I must have have finally hit on the right search t</span>
  <span id="freeText4821908249067191515" style="display:none">For years I've had a strong but vaguely-detailed memory of a book I read as a child. I couldn't remember the title, the author, the plot, or even how old I was when I read it. I knew that a boy followed his brother to a land after death, and I had a blurry image of what was possibly the cover, and I knew that younger-me had been deeply moved by it in some way. Every few years I'd try to find it, but without any luck. <br /><br />A few weeks ago, I found it. I must have have finally hit on the right search terms, or else the Wikipedia page for the book was created or updated since my last attempt. I looked through all the covers on GoodReads, and didn't recognize any of them, but I was still 90% sure. But when I ordered a used copy sight-unseen from AbeBooks, it arrived with what I instantly knew was the right cover. (I added this edition and cover to GoodReads.)<br /><br />So anyway, I saved it for a free Saturday, and finally just sat down and read it cover-to-cover. I felt like I read it with two minds: the older-me watching the younger-me rediscovering it with a sense of awe. It's unusual and beautiful and simple and epic all at once. <br /><br />The relationship between the two brothers is very sweet, and the contrast between the two boys themselves lets them illustrate an ideal form of brotherly love from two different angles. Any kid who's a sibling should read this book if only for that. Another major theme is bravery. Jonathan is the hero of the events, and may seem overly idealized in his perfection. But it's Karl's story, and he's the one who manages to perform his own small acts of bravery in spite of his fear, inspired entirely by love for (and from) his brother. In fact, I would say that Jonathan represents the Guru, incarnating as a human and facing human challenges, but only for our benefit, to teach us how to be like him. Reincarnation is another theme: how death is just a transition to another life, and not to be feared. <br /><br />I honestly can't recall what I made of all this as a child, but it does make sense now why I retained such a feeling of depth and mystery about it. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4821908249067191515" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/19664584" />
    <a title="Matthew" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/309084-matthew"><img alt="Matthew" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/19664584?book_show_action=true">Apr 07, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matthew" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matthew" href="/user/show/309084-matthew">Matthew</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer19664584" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7344281870677370254">while this is technically a children's book by a children's author, the subjects are heavier than most adult novels: death, disease, loss, the afterlife, the right to react to violence with violence, family, and loyalty. everyone i spoke to about this book said the same thing: that the book illicited tears, whether out of sadness or joy or some combination of both. it is one of the most universally loved books in the author's home country of sweden: the extended version of the movie from 1977 is</span>
  <span id="freeText7344281870677370254" style="display:none">while this is technically a children's book by a children's author, the subjects are heavier than most adult novels: death, disease, loss, the afterlife, the right to react to violence with violence, family, and loyalty. everyone i spoke to about this book said the same thing: that the book illicited tears, whether out of sadness or joy or some combination of both. it is one of the most universally loved books in the author's home country of sweden: the extended version of the movie from 1977 is shown annually on national television, and particular quotes from the book are frequently on gravestones.<br /><br />i may not have ever heard of this book if it were not for a student in a class i was teaching here in switzerland, but i can't imagine having not found it eventually. all throughout reading it, it felt like i had finally revealed a universal story that everyone already inherently knows. <br /><br />don't look it up on wikipedia, since the synopsis will give away both the multitude of surprises throughout the book, starting from the very beginning. just read it sometime if you are ready to be taken on an emotional and spiritual ride.</span>

<div id="review_1360727734" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1360727734" />
    <a title="Krista Claudine Baetiong" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13624574-krista-claudine-baetiong"><img alt="Krista Claudine Baetiong" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1552474554p2/13624574.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1360727734?book_show_action=true">Aug 11, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Krista Claudine Baetiong" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Krista Claudine Baetiong" href="/user/show/13624574-krista-claudine-baetiong">Krista Claudine Baetiong</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13624574-krista-claudine-baetiong?shelf=author-europe">author-europe</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13624574-krista-claudine-baetiong?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13624574-krista-claudine-baetiong?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13624574-krista-claudine-baetiong?shelf=childrens">childrens</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13624574-krista-claudine-baetiong?shelf=literary-accolades">literary-accolades</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1360727734" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9981149616776701030">A tale about brotherly love and finding courage even in the most difficult of circumstances, <i> The Brothers Lionheart</i> narrates the adventures of two brothers, Jonathan and Karl Lionheart, as they dwell in an idyllic happy place called Nangiyala and confront the evil that starts to threaten the peace of the land.<br/><br/>Not long after Karl joins his older brother Jonathan in a secured border in Nangiyala called Cherry Farm, he learns that a cruel tyrant named Tengil has taken control of its adjacent neigh</span>
  <span id="freeText9981149616776701030" style="display:none">A tale about brotherly love and finding courage even in the most difficult of circumstances, <i> The Brothers Lionheart</i> narrates the adventures of two brothers, Jonathan and Karl Lionheart, as they dwell in an idyllic happy place called Nangiyala and confront the evil that starts to threaten the peace of the land.<br /><br />Not long after Karl joins his older brother Jonathan in a secured border in Nangiyala called Cherry Farm, he learns that a cruel tyrant named Tengil has taken control of its adjacent neighbor, Wild Rose Valley, and is merely waiting for the right moment when he can finally seize Cherry Farm and lay claim over the entire Nangiyala region. His brother has long joined the rebel forces to free Wild Rose Valley of tyranny and thwart Tengil’s evil plan. Karl is cowardly and has looked up to Jonathan for strength all his life, but his fear for his brother’s safety far outweighs the fear he feels for Tengil, and he soon joins Jonathan in the revolt. The rebellion ends up victorious, but not without several unhappy upshots that depict torture, betrayal, self-sacrifice and death. I also thought the book carries a political tone with its strong assertion of a radical change (through people’s war) in most part of the story; however, the ending confirms that it still clings on to the ideal.<br /> <br />There is something personal that endears me to this book: perhaps it reminds me of the own utopia I often sought out as a child whenever I felt lonely or scared or just wanted a sense of contentment, and where my definition of happiness is a room full of scented stationeries or having the ability to fly like Peter Pan. It reminds me of the realization I’d been hard-pressed to accept as a child—that I didn’t have enough money then to fill even a small box with stationeries, or that Peter Pan is only real in fantasyland. Or maybe, it also reminds me that valor can burn in the heart of even the most weak-hearted person like Karl. <br /><br />But this book mostly tells us not to pine for misery. It tells us that in order to get out of a wretched situation, it needs to be struggled with; that any person should have the courage to do whatever is necessary and to deal with whatever evil he or she faces, however difficult or precarious the situation. “There are things you have to do; otherwise, you are not a human being but just a bit of filth,” so says Jonathan Lionheart. <br /><br />Despite some serious overtones, <i>The Brothers Lionheart</i> is still a children’s book through and through with its moral values and the promise that there is a beautiful land of “campfires and sagas” waiting for us in the afterlife. It is a book I actually wished I had discovered early on; perhaps it could have saved me a lot of those Sylvia Plath quirks and feeling "just a bit of filth" growing up.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9981149616776701030" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_691952393" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/691952393" />
    <a title="Orinoco Womble (tidy bag and all)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20738920-orinoco-womble-tidy-bag-and-all"><img alt="Orinoco Womble (tidy bag and all)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1406361683p2/20738920.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/691952393?book_show_action=true">Aug 11, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Orinoco Womble (tidy bag and all)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Orinoco Womble (tidy bag and all)" href="/user/show/20738920-orinoco-womble-tidy-bag-and-all">Orinoco Womble (tidy bag and all)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780192729040" href="/book/show/6875328-the-brothers-lionheart">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer691952393" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3117029843856270918">Another reviewer starts their review saying, <i>"If you don't like this book, you just don't understand anything."</i> Well, that's one reader's opinion. Rather than be that harsh, I would say that if, like me, you grew up exclusively on the Pippi stories, <b>The Brothers Lionheart</b> will be rather a surprise. It's nothing like the Longstocking books, except in the fact that the children make their own decisions and take their own destinies in hand. From the moment that Jonathan saves his brother in a fire,</span>
  <span id="freeText3117029843856270918" style="display:none">Another reviewer starts their review saying, <i>"If you don't like this book, you just don't understand anything."</i> Well, that's one reader's opinion. Rather than be that harsh, I would say that if, like me, you grew up exclusively on the Pippi stories, <b>The Brothers Lionheart</b> will be rather a surprise. It's nothing like the Longstocking books, except in the fact that the children make their own decisions and take their own destinies in hand. From the moment that Jonathan saves his brother in a fire, both boys start to learn that "bravery" actually means "doing it scared."<br /><br />My mother used to say that if a children's book can't hold the interest of an adult, it probably won't be very interesting for children either, since children aren't stupid, they are just younger and shorter. There is plenty in this book to interest and intrigue the thinking adult--or the thinking child. However, it deals with a lot of tough topics such as death, tyranny and pain (both physical and emotional) so I would perhaps recommend it for older children, say 10-12. It's a quest story, an adventure story, and it even has a dragon and a seamonster. However the narration is a bit patchy in places, jumping over parts that I would have liked explained a bit better.<br /><br />I have to say that if I'd read this back in the days when I was laughing over Pippi's antics, I wouldn't have liked it at all--no, not even in 1973, when I was 11. Now, it made a good read. If a child you know and love reads it, make sure they know you're available to discuss it...you may be surprised.<br /><br />I had no idea Ms Lindgren had written so many books; how I wish more of them were available to me.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3117029843856270918" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer5116971722253315275">It has been a while since I've read this book. But it still deserves a review from me. The writing style is gripping and the characters endearing. The message of familial love is well writ and heart wrenching. Now, that was the good part. I warn you that there will be a spoiler, and a scathing review next, because no reader should venture into this book unwarned and I suggest you look closely at this book before passing it onto a child: <a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[The main characters are both brothers.   <span id="freeText5116971722253315275" style="display:none">It has been a while since I've read this book. But it still deserves a review from me. The writing style is gripping and the characters endearing. The message of familial love is well writ and heart wrenching. Now, that was the good part. I warn you that there will be a spoiler, and a scathing review next, because no reader should venture into this book unwarned and I suggest you look closely at this book before passing it onto a child: <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[The main characters are both brothers. After they die, they both go to a fantasy land, where they have a wonderful adventure and then--now that one of them is crippled--decide to go on to 'the next land.' So they both jump off of a cliff! Although the familial love in this book is refreshing, the heavy undertones of suicide are not. Beware of this book for children, only adults who can handle this subject and reason well should venture into this read. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/125150-snail-in-danger-sid-nicolaides?shelf=children-ya">children-ya</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer388683072" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6299050628760056304">"It's Valhalla — you know, for kids!" (i.e., the heroes' afterlife, but in a somewhat toned down and sanitized way.)<br/><br/>I can see why some people fell in love with this book when they read it as children. I also think that this is a book you have to read as a child to fully relate to its charm. (There will be adults who are exceptions to this, I suspect. It depends on how you feel about death and dying. Because it deals with these themes it would be good to preview it before giving to a young child,</span>
  <span id="freeText6299050628760056304" style="display:none">"It's Valhalla — you know, for kids!" (i.e., the heroes' afterlife, but in a somewhat toned down and sanitized way.)<br /><br />I can see why some people fell in love with this book when they read it as children. I also think that this is a book you have to read as a child to fully relate to its charm. (There will be adults who are exceptions to this, I suspect. It depends on how you feel about death and dying. Because it deals with these themes it would be good to preview it before giving to a young child, I think.) The idea of it is interesting, but parts of it are simplistic, in ways that didn't satisfy me.<br /><br />This edition has lovely illustrations. Some of them evoke the old Norse style without fully imitating it.</span>

<div id="review_2882621079" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2882621079" />
    <a title="Mady" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/61441933-mady"><img alt="Mady" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1504529447p2/61441933.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2882621079?book_show_action=true">Jul 12, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mady" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mady" href="/user/show/61441933-mady">Mady</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 29657982" href="/book/show/918749.Br_derna_Lejonhj_rta">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/61441933-mady?shelf=my-opinion">my-opinion</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/61441933-mady?shelf=books-i-own">books-i-own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/61441933-mady?shelf=audiobooks">audiobooks</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/61441933-mady?shelf=swedish">swedish</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2882621079" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8297020148346950723">Wow it's really hard to rate this book. It was a first sad, then adventurous story with a WTF moment at the very end. <br/><br/>I read 'Bröderna Lejonhjärta' in Swedish while at the same time listening to the audiobook recorded by Astrid Lindgren herself, which made it enjoyable as well as a helpful learning device to become better in speaking and understanding the Swedish language.<br/><br/>I must say that I've reread 'Ronja Rövardotter' last year and it will always be my favorite Astrid Lindgren book, but then ag</span>
  <span id="freeText8297020148346950723" style="display:none">Wow it's really hard to rate this book. It was a first sad, then adventurous story with a WTF moment at the very end. <br /><br />I read 'Bröderna Lejonhjärta' in Swedish while at the same time listening to the audiobook recorded by Astrid Lindgren herself, which made it enjoyable as well as a helpful learning device to become better in speaking and understanding the Swedish language.<br /><br />I must say that I've reread 'Ronja Rövardotter' last year and it will always be my favorite Astrid Lindgren book, but then again that might be due to nostalgia because I've known the story since I was a little kid. However, 'The Brothers Lionheart' for now will be on the second place until I'm going to Sweden to buy more of Astrid Lindgren's books.<br /><br />Don't read on further if you don't know the story and the way it ends because this will be a spoiler otherwise if you ever plan to read it!<br />I found their basically suicide at the end a bit concerning considering it to be a children's story. I mean I know that Astrid Lindgren wanted to give children a kind of reassurance that there will be something waiting after death and nobody just disappears but Jonathan encouraging Karl to jump to his death because there's something better waiting afterwards is going a step to far for me. I understand that the life Jonathan would be living with his dragon caused paralysis would be a hard one but didn't the whole story serve to show us that they could get through everything as long as they stay together? Is a suicide pact really the right solution to the problem that should be presented to young children?<br />I know that often times children are smarter than we are giving them credit for and probably understand stories on a different level than adults do but I personally would be very careful to read this story to a child without explaining that there's one special unique life each of us is living and since nobody 100 percent knows what comes afterwards we maybe shouldn't be too keen to find out before the time has come.<br />Yes, Karl dosen't want to live without his brother but no, he shouldn't kill himself to be reunited with him. I know that's not the essential message or moral of the story but if we look at this magical story in a more realistic way this might come across as at least a little problematic.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8297020148346950723" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText602453695678684747" style="display:none">My best friend in the fourth grade, Dell Simpson, gave me this book and I still have his copy. Actually, I think he loaned it to me, so this goes to show that you should never loan me a book. Hear that, Multnomah County Library? Take away my card now! Also, Dell, if you're reading this, you can't have it back. It looks like I read it in the shower anyway, so you probably wouldn't want it now. <br /><br />Anyway, it's a melodramatic tale of a young, very ill boy whose older brother tells him stories about the wonderful land he will go to after he dies. Tragically, his brother dies first, when their house catches on fire. But soon the brothers, whole and in perfect health, are reunited in Wild Rose Valley in Nangiyala, a seemingly idyllic village. But all is not as it seems...and soon the brothers are embarking on a dangerous and heroic adventure...<br /><br />While I was in the Netherlands this summer, I struck up a conversation with a children's book store owner about Astrid Lindgren and how only her Pippi books are popular in the United States. He said that Pippi was ok, but the really good one was The Brothers Lionheart. I begged to differ--the Brothers Lionheart is great, but Ronia the Robber's Daughter is definitely number one in my book...but that's a topic for another review. </span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer1295817247" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12966576408661686660">This was a really sweet story about Karl "Rusky" Lionheart and his quest to be brave. A sickly boy in life, it is only after his death that his journey begins. He is reunited with his brother in Ningiyama, the land of sagas and adventures. Life seems idyllic to begin with, but Rusky soon learns that some adventures are dark and scary.<br/><br/>This book dealt with some pretty heavy themes for it to be purely a children's book. It deals with death, the afterlife, loss, disease, family, violence, and so muc</span>
  <span id="freeText12966576408661686660" style="display:none">This was a really sweet story about Karl "Rusky" Lionheart and his quest to be brave. A sickly boy in life, it is only after his death that his journey begins. He is reunited with his brother in Ningiyama, the land of sagas and adventures. Life seems idyllic to begin with, but Rusky soon learns that some adventures are dark and scary.<br /><br />This book dealt with some pretty heavy themes for it to be purely a children's book. It deals with death, the afterlife, loss, disease, family, violence, and so much more. But the overarching theme of brotherhood, and the affection and devotion that one can only receive through their siblings, hit me deep. </span>
  <a data-text-id="12966576408661686660" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('gwid7rFGxyQAmi16MosElUMCk7xPXiKrRehCgNrL9K8JdAMHVaTec7CuWt1oC6Ecfp3UtImAFG18FFtZ8bBMHg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

  }

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer12439857390572062299">Astrid Anna Emilia Lindgren, née Ericsson, (1907 - 2002) was a Swedish children's book author and screenwriter, whose many titles were translated into 85 languages and published in more than 100 countries. She has sold roughly 165 million copies worldwide. Today, she is most remembered for writing the <i>Pippi Longstocking</i> books, as well as the <i>Karlsson-on-the-Roof</i> book series.<br/><br/>Awards:<br/>Hans Christian A</span>
  <span id="freeText12439857390572062299" style="display:none">Astrid Anna Emilia Lindgren, née Ericsson, (1907 - 2002) was a Swedish children's book author and screenwriter, whose many titles were translated into 85 languages and published in more than 100 countries. She has sold roughly 165 million copies worldwide. Today, she is most remembered for writing the <i>Pippi Longstocking</i> books, as well as the <i>Karlsson-on-the-Roof</i> book series.<br /><br />Awards:<br />Hans Christian Andersen Award for Writing (1958)</span>
